Abstract
This thesis explores the efпђБcacy of Wasserstein-based distributional robust counterparts
(W-DRC) relative to sample average approximation (SAA) in active portfolio management.
The research focuses on enhancing portfolio performance through distributionally robust
strategies by limiting the bias introduced by optimizerвАЩs curse.
Two primary portfolio optimization models are considered: (i) Maximizing risk-
adjusted excess return above the S&P 500, and (ii) tracking the S&P 500 and пђБxed targets
above it. These models are formulated within a single-period setting and tested against
the SAA approach, using 23 years of historical prices from S&P 500 constituents. Due to
missing prices for certain constituents, the results are not directly transferable, but merely
serve to benchmark W-DRC against SAA.
The study reveals that W-DRC can outperform SAA in terms of out-of-sample perfor-
mance, particularly when portfolios are constructed using shorter past data spans. For the
return-based index tracking model (ITM), increased performance is observed with a quarter
of past data, while for the risk-adjusted excess return model (RAERM), an advantage is
seen with up to one year of data. The advantage diminishes when a larger database is
available, which in general lead to better out-of-sample performance. In addition, it is
shown that the improved out-of-sample performance of W-DRC relative to SAA primarily
comes from the ability to reduce the relative excess risk. Therefore, the effectiveness of
W-DRC varies with the investorвАЩs risk proпђБle, underscoring the need for case-by-case
application.
This research contributes to the пђБeld of stochastic optimization in portfolio manage-
ment, offering insights into the practical application of distributionally robust strategies.
While it is a valid criticism that statistical or black-box forecasts should be used instead
of historical prices, it does not impact the ability to compare W-DRC and SAA relatively.
However, it cannot be ruled out that the introduction of another database might change the
extend to which W-DRC is advantageous.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
The surge in available data, coupled with advancements in technology, has transformed
decision-making in general, and portfolio construction is no exception. BrieпђВy speaking,
portfolio construction consists of two stages: In the пђБrst stage, the portfolio manager must
decide on a group of investment opportunities. In the second stage, the manager must
decide on how much capital that should be allocated to each opportunity, which can be
viewed as a classical optimization task relative to a pre-determined objective. The process
is depicted in Figure 1.1. Increased computational capability has allowed for more assets to
be considered in the optimization task. Consequently, the selection process is becoming a
product of the weighting task in itself (left-pointing arrow), where a deselection correspond
to no allocation of capital.
MSFT

Weighting
AAPL
AMZN
NVDA

AAPL
Figure 1.1: Illustration of portfolio construction. For all practical purposes, the managers
start by limiting the number of assets considered. Then the remaining assets are assigned a
weight according to their individual signiпђБcance in reaching the investment objective.
Automation of portfolio construction based on model insights from various data sources
has the potential to beneпђБt both institutional and retail investors. The beneпђБts could be
1
==PAGE:16==

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
reduced fees and higher reliability, but also increased access to products offering exposure
to new asset classes and markets.
1.1
Motivation and Objective
In the realm of decision-making processes, particularly in active portfolio management
characterized by stochastic input variables, practitioners are often confronted with the
challenge of incomplete information regarding the underlying distributions. Despite
employing estimators that are consistent and unbiased in order to uncover the distributional
characteristics, the inevitable presence of variance has the potential to compromise the
integrity of the decision-making framework. This issue assumes signiпђБcant importance in
the context of classical optimization strategies that rely on these estimated characteristics.
Empirical evidence suggests that such strategies often yield results that are suboptimal on
an average basis and tend to exhibit an excessively optimistic bias. In Smith and Winkler
(2006), the authors denote this phenomenon optimizerвАЩs curse. The optimistic bias is called
the post-decision disappointment, which means that the ex-ante view is too optimistic
relative to the ex-post realization.

Figure 1.2 shows 10 projects with estimated means (known to decision-maker) and
true mean (not know by decision-maker). Suppose the task is to select the 5 best projects
and combine them into an equally weighted portfolio. This corresponds to ordering the
projects based on their estimated means and selecting the 5 largest of them. The ordered
projects are displayed in Figure 1.3. The optimal portfolio is the best possible combination
of the true means, the in-sample estimate is what we expect from the chosen project
portfolio based on the estimated means, the out-of-sample estimate is the performance of
the selected portfolio based on the true means, and the post-decision disappointment is the
gap between the in-sample performance and out-of-sample performance. It is seen that
==PAGE:17==
1.1. MOTIVATION AND OBJECTIVE
3
the selected portfolio is suboptimal as the out-of-sample performance is lower than the
optimal portfolio.
Although one cannot hope to achieve the optimal portfolio, it is naturally of great
interest to limit the gap between this and the out-of-sample performance of the chosen
project portfolio. In addition, limiting the post-decision disappointment will increase
reliability and give a more realistic view about the performance one can actually obtain.
For this reason, coping with optimizerвАЩs curse is of great interest. Finding effective ways to
reduce the impact of the curse will not only result in more realistic expectations of future
performance, it also has the potential to increase it. The hope of this thesis is to shed light
on an effective reformulation in the context of stochastic optimization, which seek to lift
the curse. This paradigm, known as distributionally robust optimization, is tested in the
context of (simple) actively managed portfolios, where the individual shares are selected
among the constituents from the S&P 500 index. SpeciпђБcally, two cases are considered:
(i) Best risk-adjusted excess return above S&P 500
(ii) Best possible tracking of S&P 500 and пђБxed targets above it
The пђБrst is very similar to a classical portfolio optimization problem, but instead of
considering absolute returns focus is shifted to the excess returns above S&P 500. In the
second case, the goal is to track S&P 500 and an enhanced version, where the returns are
shifted upwards. This setting corresponds to having a пђБxed target for the excess returns
above S&P 500. Both models fall within the following formulation:
min
w
max
Q≈УP EQ[f(w, вАЇ)]
(1.1a)
s.t.
w ≈У W
(1.1b)
The function f(w, вАЇ) denotes a loss function, which in the пђБrst case is a weighted
sum of their excess returns and conditional value-at-risk (CVaR) relative to S&P 500.
In the second case, the objective becomes to minimize the absolute value of the excess
returns and their CVaR. The set of candidate distributions P is denoted the ambiguity set,
and the uncertainties вАЇ are assumed to reside in an uncertainty set ќЮ. By selecting the
worst-case distribution in P, the portfolio becomes robust to changes in the underlying
distribution. To keep things in a relatively simple framework allowing for a more in-depth
exploration of distributional robustness, the investigation of the portfolios is restricted to a
single-period setting.
==PAGE:18==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.2
Literature Review
There exists a vast amount of literature dedicated to the subject of distributionally
robust optimization and portfolio optimization. Due to the appealing intuition with respect
to decision-making, the advantages of the distributionally robust optimization are often
exempliпђБed through simulated portfolio optimization problems. This review positions
the thesis in literature, but the theoretical treatment of the subjects is brief. Details of
distributionally robust optimization will be treated in Section 2.4.3.
In Esfahani and Kuhn (2015), the authors develop a formulation that is robust with
respect to distributions within pre-determined radius вАШ of a chosen reference distribution.
This effectively constitute a hedge against the missing information about the true, underly-
ing distribution. The set of considered distributions is denoted the ambiguity set P, and the
uncertainties вАЇ are assumed to reside in a pre-determined set ќЮ denoted the uncertainty set.
The distance between the probability distributions is measured in terms of the Wasserstein
distance, which is a more general case of the Kantorovich-Rubinstein metric. The authors
show that the distributionally robust counterpart of an expected-value program, where the
loss function is determined as the maximum of afпђБne functions, leads to a linear program
for particular choices of the uncertainty set and norms used to deпђБne the Wasserstein
distance.
Instead of constructing an ambiguity set based on the distance to some reference
distribution, one could restrict the considered distributions to have similar moments
regardless of the distances. An example of such a study is Delage and Ye (2010), where
the authors derive a semi-deпђБnite formulation of the distributionally robust optimization
problem with moment uncertainties.
The formulation in Esfahani and Kuhn (2015) is very general. It puts no restrictions
on the ambiguity set apart from the distance вАШ, and therefore it is an apparent risk that
the considered distributions are unrealistic. To cope with this, Gao and Kleywegt (2017)
proposes an ambiguity set combining the idea of using Wasserstein distance and restrictions
of the second-order moment. Under similar assumptions as the one put forward in Esfahani
and Kuhn (2015), they show that the counterpart can be represented as a semi-deпђБnite
program. In essense, this merges the ideas from both Delage and Ye (2010) and Esfahani
and Kuhn (2015).
The assumptions used in Esfahani and Kuhn (2015) and Gao and Kleywegt (2017)
to derive the tractable formulations are satisпђБed in the bi-objective program seeking
to optimize expected return while minimizing the conditional value-at-risk. For this
reason, both papers used such an example to demonstrate the power of their respective
formulations.
The idea of index tracking and enhanced index tracking is explored in respectively
==PAGE:19==
1.3. RESEARCH QUESTIONS
5
Strub and Baumann (2017) and Guastaroba (2020). Strub and Baumann (2017) considers
both deviations in returns from the benchmark as well as deviation from the trajectory,
while Guastaroba (2020) adopts a risk-reward view and focus only on deviations in the
return. Neither of the authors consider the problem that past returns reveal only partial
information about the underlying distributions.
The examined models in this thesis fall within the classical mean-risk framework, but
instead of considering a stochastic optimization approach where the sample-averages are
used to make the decisions, the reformulation proposed in Esfahani and Kuhn (2015) is
used. Despite the above mentioned disadvantages, this choice is made out of necessity. In
the context of investigating the practical beneпђБts of distributionally robust optimization on
S&P 500, there is a need for a very efпђБcient model formulation to lay the foundation for a
comprehensive analysis. As the counterparts in Esfahani and Kuhn (2015) can formulated
as linear programs under certain conditions, it is only natural to pursue this path. The
formulations of the models follow the return-based setup provided by Guastaroba (2020),
but to be able to accurately describe the loss function as the maximum of piecewise afпђБne
functions, a bi-objective view is maintained. Hence, the models can only provide results in
a Pareto optimal sense.
1.3
Research Questions
While Esfahani and Kuhn (2015) and other referenced studies employ well-controlled,
low-dimensional simulations to validate their models, the real-world applicability of
these results in high-dimensional settings remains an open question. Given the non-
stationary nature and fat-tailed distributions of equity returns, direct extrapolation of these
пђБndings to more complex environments is not straightforward. The difпђБculty lies in the
fact that these underlying distributions are never fully exposed; they are only partially
observable. Continuously collecting more data for sampling does not resolve this issue, as
the distributions themselves evolve over time. Additionally, adapting the model to focus
on index tracking, which requires only minor adjustments, raises the question of whether
such a reformulated model can enhance tracking performance. Consequently, this thesis
aims to address the following research questions:
(i) In a single-period setting with large-scale real data, how does reformulating portfolio
optimization tasks with distributional robustness impact the portfolio composition
and its out-of-sample performance? Is there an optimal amount of historical data to
consider for maximized effectiveness?
(ii) Does backtesting, both when the target is to track an enhanced version of an index
==PAGE:20==
6
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
and beat it in a risk-adjusted sense, provide supportive evidence for the effectiveness
of distributionally robust portfolio strategies?
1.4
Contributions
This thesis primarily contributes to the analysis of the linear counterparts of Esfahani
and Kuhn (2015) within a large-scale, high-dimensional context, utilizing actual price data
from the S&P 500 constituents. To my knowledge, this represents a novel empirical study
exploring the practical advantages of applying this speciпђБc distributionally robust counter-
part in such a setting. In addition to the empirical investigations, this work introduces an
innovative return-based tracking formulation. While it is inпђВuenced by Guastaroba (2020),
this particular formulation has not been previously proposed.
SigniпђБcant effort has been invested in elucidating the underlying theory and delineating
the procedure for deriving distributionally robust counterparts as per Esfahani and Kuhn
(2015). It is my hope that this will facilitate a deeper understanding and application of the
theory for researchers and practitioners who are interested in pursuing similar avenues.
1.5
Outline of Structure
To guide the reader, the structure of the thesis is outlined: Chapter 2 provides an
overview of the relevant background theory, focusing on index construction, modern
portfolio theory, risk measures, and stochastic optimization, with particular emphasis
on distributionally robust optimization. Chapters 3 and 4 detail the derivation of the
Index Tracking Model (ITM) and the Risk-Adjusted Excess Return Model (RAERM),
respectively. These chapters comprehensively address the process of deriving the linear
distributionally robust counterpart, as proposed by Esfahani and Kuhn (2015), for a
classical stochastic optimization model. Chapter 5, the empirical section, conducts two
types of analyses to address the research questions:
(i) An examination of the impacts on portfolio compositions, out-of-sample perfor-
mances, and selected metrics due to variations in the Wasserstein radius вАШ.
(ii) A comparative analysis of the distributionally robust portfolio models and their
sample-average approximation counterparts using a standard backtesting approach.
Chapter 6 concludes the thesis by summarizing the key пђБndings from the analyses. For
readers already familiar with the concepts presented in the background theory, this section
may be skipped without hindering the understanding of subsequent sections.

CHAPTER 2
Background Theory
This chapter introduces the theoretical and technical background related to the construc-
tion of equity indices, the use of mean-risk optimization frameworks, theoretical properties
of risk measures in general with a focus on a linear formulation of conditional value-at-risk
(CVaR), and lastly selected formulations within distributionally robust optimization (DRO).
The goal is to provide the reader with a stand-alone description of the most important
aspects related to the derivation of the models and general understanding of problem which
these formulations try to solve. While the following chapters are written in a way such that
they do not have to be read in the same order as they appear, it still constitutes the most
natural order.
2.1
Construction of Equity Indices
Seen through economic glasses, the beneпђБts (not necessarily пђБnancially) obtained from
the investment should be better or equal to any existing alternatives. Therefore, portfolio
construction is (often) based on a relativistic view. In the end, the objective is to obtain a
satisfactory performance relative to a benchmark. In the context of equity investing, the
benchmark is often chosen to be an equity index, which is a weighted combination of
stocks belonging to a particular market, industry or segment. Equity indices are often used
as a proxy for the overall пђБnancial and economic conditions, and while there exist a lot of
them, the aristocrats (S&P 500, Dow Jones Industrial Average, Nasdaq Composite) among
them are usually referred to as "the market". In Indices (2023a), Standard & PoorвАЩs Dow
Jones Indices (S&P DJI) divide their equity indices into two broad categories:
(i) Market Capitalization Weighted
(ii) Non-Market Capitalization Weighted Indices
9
==PAGE:24==
10
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND THEORY
As the nomenclature discloses, market capitalization weighted indices are constructed
by weighting the individual assets according to their proportion of total market value, while
non-market capitalization weighted indices are typically equally-weighted or weighted
according to their share prices.

The famous S&P 500 is an instance of a пђВoat-adjusted market capitalization weighted
index. To exemplify how the weights of the S&P 500 constituents are calculated, the
procedure for determining the weights of Apple Inc. (AAPL) on September 30, 2023
is depicted in Figure 2.1. The outstanding shares in the пђБnancial statement are given in
1000вАЩs, and the market capitalization of S&P 500 is the sum of the market capitalization of
all constituents. The пђВoat-adjustment has not been included, but it serves to exclude the
outstanding shares which are not available to the public. Such restricted shares could be
holdings by insiders. According to Blitzer (2013), the average пђВoat-adjustment factor was
around 97% in 2013 meaning 3% of the outstanding shares were considered restricted.
2.1.1
Trading an Index
While S&P 500 (and other indices) cannot be traded directly, a vast amount of products
are based on it. A very popular type of product is an exchange traded fund (ETF). On an
overall level, these follow one of two strategies:
(i) Passive Portfolio Management
(ii) Active Portfolio Management
==PAGE:25==
2.2. MODERN PORTFOLIO THEORY
11
In passive portfolio management, the objective is to obtain the exact returns of S&P
500. Due to high liquidity in the constituents (and thus low transaction costs) this often
means constructing a replicating portfolio by buying shares in the same proportion as
their individual weights in the index. In this case, one could view the index itself as the
decision-maker.
Contrary to the approach of exact tracking, active portfolio management seek to
provide better returns than the index itself, and the portfolio manager becomes the effective
decision-maker. In a variant of this, the investor is still interested in obtaining similar
returns as S&P 500, but due to certain environmental, social, and corporate governance
(ESG) commitments or similar constraints some stocks must be excluded rendering a full
replication impossible. The models introduced in the subsequent chapters fall within active
portfolio management, and S&P 500 will serve as the benchmark.
2.2
Modern Portfolio Theory
The idea of diversifying investments is not new. In fact, investment trusts and di-
versiпђБcation considerations predate the theory of investments themselves by centuries
[Markowitz (1999)]. Anecdotal evidence is found in ShakespeareвАЩs work. In Markowitz
(1999), the author quotes from the Merchant of Venice (written in 1598), where the
merchant Antonio says:
My ventures are not in one bottom trusted,
Nor to one place; nor is my whole estate
Upon the fortune of this present year;
Therefore, my merchandise makes me not sad.
Merchant Antonio in Merchant of Venice (Act I, Scene I)
Intuition paved the way for formally deпђБning the art of composing portfolios. On
the basis of Markowitz (1952), for which he received the Nobel Prize in 1990, modern
portfolio theory (MPT) is often contributed to Harry M. Markowitz. In this section, a few
key results from the general theory in the пђБeld is outlined. This will lay the foundation for
the intuition behind the models which will be introduced in later sections.
2.2.1

Under the new construction, the set of dominant portfolios are denoted the capital
market line (CML). As seen from Figure 2.2, it is a straight line with intercept at the
risk-free rate r0. It is tangent to the efпђБcient frontier (EF) at a particular portfolio called
the tangency portfolio or market portfolio. As it delivers the highest possible risk-adjusted
return in terms of the Sharpe ratio, the theory states that any investor will hold a portfolio
consisting of the market portfolio and the risk-free rate, where the weighting between them
is adjusted according to the investorвАЩs risk-aversion. However, as not all investors have
equal access to leveraging positions due to margin and leverage constraints, it has been
shown that the CML is less steep in reality. This observation has given rise to the betting
against beta strategy. See Frazzini and Pedersen (2014) for further details.

and cardinality constraints are considered. While other important portfolio constraints
such as the modelling of transaction costs will be omitted, as they have no relevance with
respect to the models introduced in subsequent chapters, a few can be found in Appendix
G.3.

2.2.3
Summary of Modern Portfolio Theory
In this section, I have brieпђВy introduced the concept of portfolio optimization with the
mean-variance framework originally developed by Markowitz. In addition, I considered
a few portfolio constraints that are encountered in the real world applications. A bold
assumption so far has been complete knowledge of ¬µ and ќ£. However, this is never the
case, and therefore the presented formulations have little use in practice. The reason
this section has been included is because the main topic of the thesis is to incorporate
the uncertainty into our selection process. Hence, the objective is to hedge against the
distributional risk that is inherent to the portfolio optimization task. The idea is abstract,
but an attempt to outline the details is provided in Subsection 2.4.3.
2.3
Risk Measures
The quantiпђБcation of risk is important for making sound and responsible decisions
under uncertainty. In the context of the пђБnancial industry, it is particularly important as
regulators need to specify adequate capital requirements to secure the overall stability
==PAGE:32==
18
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND THEORY
of the sector. In this section, important deпђБnitions with relation to risk measures are
summarized.
Consider the probability space (вД¶, F, P), where вД¶denote the set of possible outcomes,
F is the вА°-algebra denoting the set of possible events, and P is a probability measure.
Consider the space of F-measurable random variables L. Let X ≈У L represent random
variables for which X(√К) is a пђБnancial payoff under the outcome √К ≈У вД¶. Let X(√К) > 0
denote a пђБnancial gain, and let X(√К) < 0 denote a пђБnancial loss. Based on DeпђБnition 2.1
in Artzner et al. (1999), a risk measure is then deпђБned as the mapping
√Н : L вАШ√¶ R
(2.14)
satisfying normalization, translational invariance, and monotonicity (see DeпђБnition
1). Hence, a risk measure aggregates information about a random variable X (possibly
an entire portfolio of assets) into a single number, which can be used for guidance and
decision-making. Although most people have an idea of what risk means, it is not given
which properties that the mapping √Н should satisfy in order to be meaningful.
In the following, I start by deпђБning two central risk measures, namely value-at-risk
(VaR) and conditional value-at-risk (CVaR), that will be used in the construction of the
portfolio models. After introducing the risk measures, the family of coherent and convex
risk measures is explored and discussed.
2.3.1
Value-at-Risk and Conditional Value-at-Risk
The value-at-risk (VaR) measure is one of the most well-known and adopted risk
measures. With a speciпђБed conпђБdence level denoted as вАФ (or 1 вЙ†вАУ in some literature), the
objective is to determine an upper probabilistic limit of the loss, ensuring with a certainty
of вАФ ¬Ј100% that this limit will not be exceeded. As losses are generally reported as positive
numbers, let L = вЙ†X denote the losses of a portfolio X. Then the VaR based on the
вАФ-percentile is deпђБned as
VaRвАФ(X) := вЙ†inf{x ≈У R : FX(x) > 1 вЙ†вАФ} = F вЙ†1
L (вАФ)
(2.15)
where вАФ ≈У (0, 1). As it is based on the quantiles of the loss distribution, it belongs to
the family of quantile-based risk measures.
While VaRвАФ(X) establishes the bound in вАФ ¬Ј 100% of the time, it says nothing about
what happens in the remaining (1 вЙ†вАФ) ¬Ј 100% cases. This is a genuine concern as
distributions can be multimodal or have fat tails leading to excessive losses in the case
where VaRвАФ(X) is exceeded. Hence, this put forward the following relevant question:
What happens conditional on VaRвАФ(X) being exceeded? A risk measure, that attempts

holds regardless of the considered distribution, is often used in the approximation of
chance-constraints in stochastic programming.

2.3.2
Coherent Risk Measures
In Artzner et al. (1999), the authors introduce an axiomatic framework which speciпђБes
4 desirable properties. Risk measures satisfying these 4 axioms, which are stated in
DeпђБnition 1, are called coherent.
DeпђБnition 1 (Coherent Risk Measure). A mapping √Н : L вАШ√¶ R is called a coherent risk
measure if it satisпђБes the following four axioms:
(I) Monotonicity: √Н(X) √Ж √Н(Y ) iff. P(X √Ш Y ) = 1, X, Y ≈У L.
(II) Translation Invariance: √Н(X + a) = √Н(X) вЙ†a for all X ≈У L, a ≈У R.
(III) Positive Homogeneity: √Н(вБДX) = вБД√Н(X) for all X ≈У L, вБД √Ш R.
(IV) Sub-additivity: √Н(X + Y ) √Ж √Н(X) + √Н(Y ) for all X, Y ≈У L.
The monotonicity axiom (I) states that the risk of a portfolio X is less risky than a
portfolio Y if X √Ш Y holds almost surely. The intuition is clear: If a portfolio has a larger
payoff than another in all outcomes √К ≈У вД¶for which the probability is non-zero, then it is
perceived less risky. Hence, the monotonicity axiom is a natural requirement.
The translation invariance axiom (II) states that adding a certain payoff a to a portfolio
X should reduce the risk by exactly a. This essentially means that reducing the frequency
of which пђБnancial losses occur has to reduce the risk of the portfolio.
The positive homogeneity axiom (III) states that scaling a portfolio proportionally to a
constant factor вБД should increase the risk proportionally. In words, if a portfolio consists
of a stock S, then a portfolio consisting of 2S should be twice as risky. This axiom has
been debated as it does not penalize concentration risk [Chen and Hu (2015)].
The sub-additivity axiom (IV) that a portfolio consisting of random payoffs is less risky
than holding the risky assets individually. This axiom reпђВects the notion of diversiпђБcation.

2.3.3
Convex Risk Measures
Some of the axioms in DeпђБnition 1 appear familiar. In fact, it follows directly from
positive homogeneity (III) and subadditivity (IV) that
√Н(вБДX + (1 вЙ†вБД)Y ) √Ж √Н(вБДX) + √Н((1 вЙ†вБД)Y )
(2.24a)
= вБД√Н(X) + (1 вЙ†вБД)√Н(Y )
(2.24b)
which implies that any coherent risk measure is weakly convex. This is great news with
respect to optimization purposes as this guarantees a (possibly non-unique) global extrema.
Hence, the axiom of coherency are not only intuitively appealing, they also provide a
practical advantage. It is important to mention that the implication is not bidirectional
as convex measures can fail to satisfy monotonicity (I) and translation invariance (II)
making them non-coherent. Hence, convexity in the light of risk measures is a weaker
requirement. The nature of convex risk measure, and how they can be used to induce a
family of coherent risk measures, is outlined in Chen and Hu (2015).
==PAGE:37==
2.3. RISK MEASURES
23
2.3.4

2.3.5
Summary of Risk Measures
Obtaining higher expected returns without considering levels of expected risk is not
particularly impressive. This is because higher expected returns are (generally) an indica-
tion of the excess expected risk the investor must accept. Therefore, the inherent trade-off
between the returns and the risk is at the core of any type of investment.
In this section, the ideas of coherent and convex risk measures with offset in the two
risk measures, VaR and CVaR, have been covered. These are core to the models presented
in the subsequent chapters. While VaR is not convex nor coherent, because it fails to
satisfy the subadditivity axiom (IV), CVaR is both. In addition to its appealing theoretical
properties from a risk management perspective, it also has appealing properties from
an optimization perspective. This was demonstrated by using a well-known result from
Rockafeller and Uryasev (1999) showing that minimizing CVaR of a portfolio can be
formulated as a linear program.
2.4
Mathematical Optimization
Mathematical optimization is a broad topic, and this section seeks by no means to
provide a comprehensive overview. However, in the context of motivating the use of
distributionally robust optimization (DRO), it is useful to start the journey from the basics
of stochastic programming (SP), then touch upon general robust optimization (RO), and
then пђБnally arrive at worst-case expectations under distributions conпђБned to an a priori
chosen ambiguity set.
==PAGE:39==
2.4. MATHEMATICAL OPTIMIZATION
25
2.4.1
Stochastic Programming
While it is very convenient to think of the world as deterministic, it is a gross simpliпђБ-
cation. However, some problems are better described by this approach than others, because
most information about a problem might be available at the time of the decision-making.
In others, such as portfolio optimization, the decision-maker only have partial information,
and the actual outcome of the decisions is stochastic in nature. Consider the following
optimization problem:
min
w
f(w, вАЇ)
(2.31a)
s.t.
w ≈У W
(2.31b)
where w ≈У RM represent the decision variables, W is the feasible set such that w ≈У W
is an admissible portfolio, and вАЇ ≈У RM represents the uncertain variables. Let (вД¶, F, P)
be the probability space where вД¶is the set of all outcomes √К, F is the set of all events (a
collection of outcomes), and P : F вАШ√¶ [0, 1] is the probability measure. Currently, Model
2.31 is ambiguous in the sense that, if вАЇ is continuous or countably inпђБnite, it gives rise
to an inпђБnite amount of optimization problems (one for each possible realization вАЇ(√К)
for √К ≈У вД¶). Even in the case of a пђБnite amount of optimization problems, one still need
to make a decision on which problem that is the best to solve. This motivates the need
to disambiguate the problem stated in Model 2.31. Yet, as stated on page 27 in Conejo
et al. (2010), it is not clear what the best way to do that is. A very intuitive approach is to
forget the uncertainty and optimize over the mean EP[вАЇ], which gives rise to the following
program:
min
w
f(w, EP[вАЇ])
(2.32a)
s.t.
w ≈У W
(2.32b)
The program in Model 2.32 gives rise to an expected value solution under the as-
sumption that the probability measure P is known exactly, which is of course rarely the
case. In practice, one would estimate the moments with a sample mean. This formulation
establishes the basis for the classical Markowitz framework, but as have been argued
previously, it is known to suffer from optimizerвАЩs curse as the overly optimistic results
have a large inпђВuence on the optimal decision variables w. Instead of optimizing over
the mean values of the uncertainty вАЇ, one can optimize over the expectation of the entire
objective function:
==PAGE:40==

CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND THEORY
min
w
EP[f(w, вАЇ)]
(2.33a)

w ≈У W

The program in Model 2.33 is denoted the original stochastic program. In a single-
period setting with f(w, вАЇ) being linear in вАЇ, the optimal portfolio weights w will be
the same in respectively Model 2.32 and Model 2.33. Nevertheless, the formulation in
Model 2.33 is more convenient in terms of the reformulation that will be considered in the
subsequent sections.
While single-period portfolio optimization belongs to the realm of single-stage prob-
lems, where the choices of the portfolio decisions are here-and-now decisions, stochastic
programming is often applied in a multi-stage setting, where subsequent actions, recourse
decisions, are needed based on the realizations of the uncertainty вАЇ. For the interested
reader, Shapiro and Ruszczynski (2003) and Birge and Louveaux (2010) are excellent
sources on the subject.
2.4.2
Robust Optimization
The approaches just described disambiguate Model 2.33 by adopting an expectation
operator. It is not given that this is necessarily the best approach. Depending on the
criticality of the involved system, it can be desirable to increase the reliability and provide
a solution that performs well under extreme outcomes such as six sigma events (see e.g.
Mefford et al. (2017)). Suppose that the uncertainty variables вАЇ ≈У RM resides in a known
uncertainty set U, which is compact and convex. An example of such an uncertainty set
could be a polyhedral:
U = {вАЇ ≈У RM | AвАЇ √Ж b}
(2.34)
where A ≈У RK вЧКRM, and b ≈У RK. Given any compact and convex uncertainty set,
possibly of the structure in 2.34, Model 2.31 can be robustiпђБed as follows:
min
w
max
вАЇ≈УU f(w, вАЇ)
(2.35a)
s.t.
w ≈У W
(2.35b)
which is referred to as the robust counterpart (RC). Although this for particular choices
of the uncertainty set can lead to tractable formulations, it is generally unattractive for a
several reasons: Firstly, it requires full information about the structure of U, which is not
always the case. Secondly, the price of robustifying the optimization problem might be,
==PAGE:41==
2.4. MATHEMATICAL OPTIMIZATION
27
from a subjective assessment, too high. In other words, the solution might be way too
conservative, where the degree of conservatism depend on the tightness of the uncertainty
set U. A possible solution is to use a budget of uncertainty as described in Bertsimas and
Sim (2004), where the uncertainty is only realized partially worst-case. This does however
not counter the problem of specifying the structure of the uncertainty set U in a sufпђБciently
tight manner.
While this is naturally speciпђБed in a single-stage setting, one can utilize adjustable
robust optimization for a multi-stage setting by imposing functional structures on the
decisions made in later stages. This idea is explored in detail in Gorissen et al. (2015).
Lastly, it should be mentioned that one often encounters uncertainty in the constraints
as well. These can be robustiпђБed in a similar manner by requiring that the constraints
are met for all possible realizations in the uncertainty set U. A comprehensive amount
of material exist on the subject. A good starting point is to explore Ben-Tal et al. (2009),
which provides a thorough treatment.
2.4.3
Distributionally Robust Optimization
A general concern for the robust optimization approach was the degree of conservatism
and its dependence on the ability to describe the uncertainty set sufпђБciently tight. Suppose
instead a different approach is adopted: Instead of a assuming a worst-case outcome, one
could assume a worst-case distribution of outcomes from a set of distributions. The set
of distributions is denoted the ambiguity set P. Effectively, this idea provides a hedge
against the distributionally uncertainty inherent to any data-driven stochastic program. The
idea is appealing, because in reality the distribution of the uncertainty parameters вАЇ ≈У RM
is not exactly know. In addition, if one can formalize the concept of similarity between
the distributions, then it would be possible to adjust the degree of conservatism based on
some pre-speciпђБed assessment. Formally, in line with the deпђБnitions in Zymler (2010)
and Esfahani and Kuhn (2015), the distributionally robust optimization (DRO) problem is
deпђБned as follows:
min
w
max
Q≈УP EQ[f(w, вАЇ)]
(2.36a)
s.t.
w ≈У W
(2.36b)
where вАЇ is F-measurable for all Q ≈У P. To clarify, the expectation operator is deпђБned
as
EQ[f(w, вАЇ)] =
вБД
вАЇ≈УќЮ f(w, вАЇ) dQ(вАЇ)
(2.37)

is a loss function, which the objective is to minimize. It is additionally assumed that the
true distribution P of вАЇ has support on ќЮ, which allow it to reside in P. Although not
mentioned previously, if is clear that evaluating the objective for a given choice of Q
appears to be intractable as it requires the evaluation of a multidimensional integral, which
involves an inherent time-precision trade-off. Hence, the challenges so far consists of
making tractable reformulations of 2.37 as well as deпђБning a family of distributions that is
meaningful to consider.

SpeciпђБcation of Ambiguity Sets
In order to meaningfully progress with deriving a tractable convex formulation for
2.36, a natural starting point is the speciпђБcation of the family of distributions P. In the
пђБeld of DRO, there are generally three approaches: One could formulate the ambiguity
set such that it contains probability measures deпђБned on moments similar to those of the
empirical probability measure. Alternatively, one could use a measure of distance between
a reference distribution (e.g., empirical distribution) and candidate distributions in order to
robustify the optimization problem against all candidates within a radius вАШ. Lastly, one
could adopt a mixture of the two. All the approaches rely on a pre-speciпђБed uncertainty set
ќЮ, which has been discussed under the robust optimization section. With this uncertainty
set, let M(ќЮ) to be a metric space (M, d) deпђБned on ќЮ.
The пђБrst approach is referred to as the moment-based approach. Consider the following
moment-based ambiguity set:
P = {Q ≈У M(ќЮ) | EQ[вАЇ] = ЋЖ¬µ, EQ[(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)T] = ЋЖќ£}
(2.42)
where ЋЖ¬µ and ЋЖќ£ are estimates of respectively the пђБrst-order moment and the variance-
covariance matrix. The set deпђБned in 2.42 consists of all the distributions that share
distributions with the moments similar to that of the moment estimates obtain from the
observed data. Clearly, full knowledge of the пђБrst-order and second-order moments is
a bold assumption, which is raised as a concern by Delage and Ye (2010). With good
out-of-sample performance results to a portfolio problem, they propose the following
ambiguity set:
P = {Q ≈У M(ќЮ) | (EQ[вАЇ] вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)T ЋЖќ£вЙ†1(EQ[вАЇ] вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ) √Ж вАЬ1,
(2.43)
EQ[(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)T] 4 вАЬ2 ЋЖќ£}
(2.44)
where the пђБrst requirement (EQ[вАЇ] вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)T ЋЖќ£вЙ†1(EQ[вАЇ] вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ) √Ж вАЬ1 conпђБnes the true
пђБrst-order moment of вАЇ to be in an ellipsoidal of size вАЬ1, and then second requirement
EQ[(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)T] 4 вАЬ2 ЋЖќ£ restricts the the centered second-order moment вАЇ to lie in a
positive semideпђБnite cone. Other moment-based ambiguity sets are explored in Goh and
Sim (2010). While the idea of moment-based ambiguity sets is appealing, the authors of
Gao and Kleywegt (2017) state that it tend to produce unrealistic worst-case distributions
leading to unnecessarily conservative solutions.
The second approach is denoted the distance-based approach. Consider the following
distance-based ambiguity set:
P = {Q ≈У M(ќЮ) | d(Q, P) < вАШ}
(2.45)
==PAGE:44==
30
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND THEORY
This ambiguity set is assumption-free about the moments of the candidate distributions.
Instead, it is assumed that the true distribution of our uncertainty variables вАЇ are within a
distance вАШ to the reference distribution P based on a measure of distance d. In Esfahani
and Kuhn (2015), the authors state that popular choices for the distance measure d is
Kullback-Leibler divergence, Prohorov metric, and the Wasserstein metric. Like in the
moment-based approach, the ambiguity set includes a parameter вАШ that has to be chosen a
priori to the optimization task. However, unlike the moment-based approach, choosing
вАШ = 0 yields a singleton P = {P} solving the problem under the reference distribution
P. If this distribution is chosen to be ЋЖPN, then the solution for вАШ = 0 is the classical SAA
solution, which makes the degree of conservatism truly adjustable.
The third approach is a hybrid of the above two approaches. This approach is pursued
in Gao and Kleywegt (2017) as a way to encounter their criticism of the conservatism of
the two approaches separately. While there seems to be no consensus on the naming of
this approach, it seem appropriate to denote it a mixture-based approach. Consider the
following mixture-based ambiguity set:
P = {Q ≈У M(ќЮ) | d(Q, P) < вАШ, EQ[(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)T 4 ≈ЄЋЖќ£}
(2.46)
The ambiguity set in 2.46 encodes the idea of being conпђБdent about the closeness
of the true distribution to the reference distribution P and the precision of the second-
order moment, where ≈Є is in inпђВationary parameter used to adjust the level of trust in
the estimate in a similar way to Delage and Ye (2010). The ambiguity set 2.46 is shown
to yield increased out-of-sample performance in Gao and Kleywegt (2017) compared to
using ambiguity sets that rely on only one of the above stated approaches.
In the following section, I proceed with the metric-based approach utilizing the Wasser-
stein metric as a way to quantify the distances between the reference distribution P and the
candidate distributions Q. It will be shown that, under mild assumptions, one can derive
tractable reformulations that are equivalent to Model 2.36.

2.4.5
Summary of Mathematical Optimization
In this section, stochastic programming (SP), robust optimization (RO), and distribu-
tionally robust optimization (DRO) have been outlined.
The shortcoming that SP is generally too optimistic motivated the introduction of RO,
which assumes a worst-case outcome. However, as this turn out to be a quite conservative
==PAGE:50==
36
CHAPTER 2. BACKGROUND THEORY
approach, DRO was introduced to establish a middle-ground between the optimism and
the conservatism. Instead of considering worst-case outcomes, it considers worst-case
distributions.
In the chapter, several ambiguity sets were constructed. However, the focus was
directed at a metric-based formulation using the Wasserstein distance to construct the
ambiguity set. The Wasserstein distance was brieпђВy discussed in relation to an optimal
transportation plan and a few examples were explored. After this, Corollary 5.1 of Esfahani
and Kuhn (2015) was described. In the subsequent chapters, the focus will be on the
formulation resulting from a primal norm with p = 1.
It was originally the ambition to continue the work by exploring the formulation shown
in Gao and Kleywegt (2017). However, due to the high-dimensional space on which
we will construct portfolios, it has been excluded from the analysis. For the interested
reader, the main result of the paper, which incorporate the ambiguity set 2.46, is stated in
Appendix E.
==PAGE:51==
CHAPTER 3
Return-Based Index Tracking
The overall goal of index tracking is to select a subset (possibly the entire index) of
assets whose performance imitate that of the index. Although this thesis is concerned with
equity markets only, which greatly simpliпђБes the analysis to linear dynamics, the problem
can also be extended to deal with other asset classes such as bonds, derivatives, private
equity, and more. For an excellent overview of dealing with options, the reader can consult
Zymler (2010).
3.1
The Index Tracking Problem (ITP)
In a frictionless market with no internal policies and external regulation constraining
the agent, the best tracking portfolio is obtained by full replication. This corresponds
to choosing a portfolio w such that the entries wi are equal to the weights of the index
constituents i (see example in Figure 2.1). However, markets are not frictionless, and
with each purchase and sale of assets the fund incurs variable and пђБxed costs. In very
liquid markets such as S&P 500, transaction costs are however close to negligible. Another
challenge could be the constraints imposed on the portfolios by speciпђБcally tailored SRI
and ESG policies, which are becoming increasingly popular. This means fund managers
are restricted to choose from a rather small subset of assets. The goal of index tracking is
to construct a portfolio that satisпђБes all these constraints while delivering a performance
that is as similar to the benchmark index as possible.
The central question in index tracking is that of how to measure similarity. Broadly
speaking, index tracking formulations within operations research can be separated into
return-based and value-based approaches [Strub and Baumann (2017)].
Return-based formulations seek to choose an optimal portfolios whose returns are
as similar as possible to the index returns. The idea is that this will lead to similar
normalized developments of the benchmark and the index portfolio in the evaluation
37
==PAGE:52==
38
CHAPTER 3. RETURN-BASED INDEX TRACKING
period. However, as only a subset of assets is generally selected, the returns will inevitably
deviate from the target returns, and these errors will propagate throughout the period. A
main drawback of the return-based methods is that they do not consider that the weights
of the portfolio constituents are dynamic. Hence, the weights optimized on a historical
realizations effectively represents average weights. As a consequence, this might lead the
tracking portfolio to deviate signiпђБcantly from the index that it seeks to track.
Value-based formulations take another approach. They seek to minimize the distance
between the normalized trajectories in the past. This means that past deviations from the
target trajectory is penalized implicitly, and this tend to lead to an index portfolio that is
closer in distance to the tracking index. This is however at the expense of the shape of the
normalized index portfolio trajectory. Although this method takes dynamic weights into
account, it is general concerning that the correlation between the index tracking portfolio
and the actual index is low compared to that of the return-based formulations.
Both approaches suffer from substantial rebalancing and transaction costs as these are
not typically addressed in the objective function [Strub and Baumann (2017), page 371].
In Strub and Baumann (2017), the authors propose a value-based mixed-integer linear
program using a single optimization criterion that takes the properties of both return-based
and value-based formulations into account. Although this formulation addresses a valid
criticism, the portfolio turnover (changes at each rebalancing) is not considered in this
thesis. This decision was mainly a matter of scoping the investigation.
For the purpose of testing the beneпђБts of using a distributionally robust optimization
approach, this thesis targets the return-based formulation as it yields loss functions that
a readily described as the maximum of K afпђБne functions (see Assumption 2). Instead
of considering a "real" benchmark, an enhanced index is constructed by requiring excess
returns above the regular index, increasing the complexity of the tracking task.

3.2.1
Sample Average Approximation (SAA) of Index Tracking
Model (ITM)
So far, the probability measure P has not been discussed. If it was known, it would
be possible to sample from it. This would however lead to an intractable problem as the
expectation operator, which is needed both to minimize the tracking error and conditional
value-at-risk, is a high-dimensional integral. An alternative approach is to adopt the
empirical probability measure ЋЖPN based on N observations. In the following subsections,
the expectation operators will be approximated with a SAA approach. As Model 3.8 is
currently nonlinear, the natural starting point is to reformulate it into a tractable program.
In fact, due to a simple nonlinear structure, a linear program that is equivalent can be
derived. The procedure is as follows: I start by linearizing the two terms of Model 3.8
separately for the sake of keeping the procedure manageable. These are then combined
into a single linear program that yields Pareto optimal solutions that depends on the choice
of the risk-aversion parameter пђВ.

3.2.2

(W-DRC) of Index Tracking Model (ITM)

the available historical samples, this is not the case. Modelling the exact dynamics is

3.3
Summary
This chapter introduces a distributionally robust version of a return-based index tracking
model. SpeciпђБcally, Model 3.31 maintains robustness against variations in probability
distribution within a Wasserstein radius of вАШ from the empirical distribution derived from
data. While such robust models tend to be conservative, potentially yielding suboptimal
average results, the hypothesis is that they could enhance out-of-sample performance by
mitigating the bias associated with the optimizerвАЩs curse.
A limitation of this approach is the lack of clear guidance on selecting the parameter пђВ,
as the model currently yields a set of Pareto-optimal solutions. Ideally, пђВshould align with
the investorвАЩs risk aversion, a topic not extensively explored here.
==PAGE:63==
CHAPTER 4
Risk-Adjusted Excess Returns
In this chapter, the goal is to develop a model constructing portfolios delivering the
best possible risk-adjusted excess returns relative to the benchmark. The model is very
similar to the one derived previously, and therefore the derivations will be concise.
4.1
Model Formulation
Suppose that the goal is to provide the best possible risk-adjusted excess returns relative
to S&P500. The performance is then measured in terms of an objective function, where
the goal is to maximize the trade-off between excess returns relative to the benchmark
while simultaneously minimizing the excess risk. The adopted risk measure is conditional
value-at-risk (CVaR) for the reasons outlined in earlier chapters. Formally, the program
takes the form:
min
w
EP[вЙ†Re(w)] + пђВ¬Ј CVaRP
вАФ(Re(w))
(4.1a)
s.t.
w ≈У W
(4.1b)
Model 4.1 is similar to the one considered in section 7.1 of Esfahani and Kuhn (2015).
However, instead of considering the actual returns of the portfolio, they are considered
with respect to the benchmark. The uncertainty вАЇ is therefore the daily excess returns as
deпђБned in 3.4.
4.1.1
Sample Average Approximation (SAA) of Risk-Adjusted
Excess-Return Model (RAERM)
It is possible to approximate the expectation operator of 4.1 linearly by using SAA.
Similarly, the CVaR terms can be linearized with the exact same procedure as the one

weighting reпђВecting that more recent scenarios are more likely to occur in the near-future.
However, as this would introduce yet another hyperparameter to be adjusted to provide
proper value, it is disregarded in the subsequent derivations.

==PAGE:67==
4.2. SUMMARY
53
4.2
Summary
This chapter presents the distributionally robust counterpart of a mean-CVaR model
based on daily excess returns Re(w). While robustness with respect to probability measure
might intuitively suggest more reliable yet less optimal portfolio compositions w, the
inability to fully determine probability distributions from past data suggests potential
improved out-of-sample performance. This hypothesis will be examined in the empirical
section.
As for W-DRC ITM, a limitation of this approach is the lack of clear guidance on
selecting the parameter пђВ.
==PAGE:68==
==PAGE:69==
CHAPTER 5
Evaluation of Portfolio Models
In this chapter, the objective is to assess and compare the efпђБcacy of the proposed
portfolio models. The analysis commences with an elucidation of the dataset construction,
highlighting both its advantages and drawbacks. Subsequently, I expound upon metrics
for evaluating model performance. Having established this foundation, the following
test-frameworks are introduced:
(i) A rolling-window conпђБguration, implemented with and without a validation set,
respectively.
(ii) A backtesting approach employing a dynamic constituent set and quarterly rebalanc-
ing.
The пђБrst framework serves to conduct a comprehensive sensitivity analysis, scruti-
nizing the impact of distributional uncertainty parameters on portfolio composition and
performance, particularly concerning the objective functions. The second framework
is employed to evaluate the historical performance of an investment strategy featuring
quarterly rebalancing using information available at each rebalancing instance. It has been
decided that the set of feasible portfolios W consists solely of the fully-invested constraint
and the no-shorting constraint. Formally,
W = {w ≈У RM+1 | w √Ш 0, 1Tw = 1}
(5.1)
where M denote the number of considered constituents, and the additional weight
is the allocation in a short-term bond. The details will be outlined below. The reason
for disregarding additional portfolio constraints is that it yields little to no insight about
the general impact of the Wasserstein radius вАШ while leading to a signiпђБcant increase in
the computational complexity. As already stated in Section 2.2, commonly encountered
portfolio constraints are outlined in Appendix G.3.
55
==PAGE:70==
56
CHAPTER 5. EVALUATION OF PORTFOLIO MODELS
All empirical results are obtained on a MacBook Pro (M1, 2020) with a 8-core CPU
and 8 RAM. The models are implemented in Python via MOSEKвАЩs Fusion API. The
programs have been solved using the default interior-point implementation1.
5.1
Datasets for Model Assessment on S&P 500
The task of constructing a dataset for evaluating models relative to a benchmark is
not trivial. Although пђБnancial data providers do exist, the data is not published freely.
Therefore, it was decided to use current constituents and historical adjustments as stated
on Wikipedia. The source is by no means optimal, and in retrospect an agreement with a
пђБnancial data provider should have been established prior to the beginning of the thesis
period: One such option could be SPICE offered by S&P500 Dow Jones Indices, which is
a gateway to index data. The product brochure Indices (2023b) explains the details. As
both the SAA and W-DRC models are evaluated on the same, possibly пђВawed dataset, it
should still be sufпђБcient to carry out a comparative analysis, which can shed light of the
beneпђБts of using the different approaches.
5.1.1
List of Index Constituents
Apart from the 10 largest constituents in terms of index weights, the current constituents
are not disclosed publicly by S&P Dow Jones Indices. The same holds for the historical
constituents which are necessary to carry out a thorough backtest of the methods. To
overcome the challenge, it was decided to scrape the current constituents and the historical
removals and additions to the index based on information published on Wikipedia2. As of
12/12-2023, the two tables included on the site were denoted "S&P 500 component stocks"
and "Selected changes to the list of S&P 500 components". By scraping the current tickers
with Python, it was possible to create a set of constituents and progressively update it back
in time. The result was a complete set with all tickers that are or have been part of the S&P
500 index since 01/11-1999 up until 17/11-2023.
5.1.2
Download of Adjusted Closing Prices for Index Constituents
Based on the scraped tickers, the adjusted closing prices3 for the historical and current
constituents were downloaded with a premium subscription to the stock market API offered

5.1.3
Download of U.S. Treasury Yields for Various Maturities
To incorporate the use of a risk-free rate, par yield quotes were obtained from the U.S.
Department of Treasury for all possible maturities. Par yields differ slightly from yield to
maturity (YTM). They describe the coupon rate at which a bond for a given maturity would
trade at par, whereas YTM describes the interest rate that would result in a present value
of 0 for all cash пђВows. For short maturities they are approximately equal, and therefore it
has been decided to keep the par yields although it would be most correct from a technical
view point to use YTM. For examples of how par yields are calculated, the reader can
consult Appendix B. An overview of how the historical par yield rates have developed is
shown in Figure 5.2. In addition, Figure 5.3 shows par yield curves on selected historical
dates.

Subset Construction of Index Constituents
Out of the constituents included in the index historically, not all continued to be listed

actual index constituents at the given time.
(ii) SP500RawIntersect: This dataset includes adjusted closing prices for all con-

absolute comparison, where the performance is considered relative to that of professional
funds, this is of course not satisfactory. However, as already mentioned, it should not raise
great concern in the context of comparing two models on the same dataset as they both
consider the same information.

5.2
Sensitivity to Changes in Wasserstein Radius
In order to examine the practical advantages of applying the Wasserstein-based dis-
tributionally robust optimization scheme, it is natural to start by investigating how the
==PAGE:75==

various Wasserstein radii вАШ, whose range have been selected by trial and error. Then the
performance is evaluated on a previously unseen testing set of size N = 63 corresponding
to a quarter of daily returns. The procedure is repeated by shifting the window forward
in time. Common for all experiments is that the shifting has been selected such that it
results in a total of 200 tests and that the models for all training sizes N are evaluated on
the same testing sets. The results are presented for respectively Model 3.19 (W-DRC ITM)
and Model 4.3 (W-DRC RAERM).

right limit вАШ √¶ 0) to the observed empirical distribution. It is seen that the out-of-sample

and N = 252 although the effect is barely visible for latter. In addition, the Wasserstein
radius вАШ resulting in the optimal out-of-sample performance shifts to the left reducing
the size of the considered candidate distributions in the ambiguity set. For the sample
sizes N larger than 252, the impact of a пђБxed вАШ will on average result in a worse average
out-of-sample performance. As demonstrated in Esfahani and Kuhn (2015), this would be
expected if the samples were drawn from a stationary distribution, where more samples
would yield a higher certainty about the underlying structure. However, for the non-
==PAGE:79==
5.2. SENSITIVITY TO CHANGES IN WASSERSTEIN RADIUS
65
stationary case where the moments of the underlying distribution are time-varying, it was
hypothesized that an optimal training size N would exist following the idea that recent
market data more accurately reпђВect the near-future characteristics. A possible explanation
is that historical data for large N in itself constitutes a reasonable worst-case distribution.
Hence, if more than 252 days of daily returns are available, a пђБxed Wasserstein radius
вАШ gives no clear beneпђБts in terms of average performance measured by the value of the
objective. Hence, the optimal choice for N appear to be unbounded within the investigated
range although it exhibits decreasing marginal beneпђБts.

of the objective, it would be expected that it only affects the magnitude of the beneпђБt and
not the presence of the beneпђБt itself.
5.3
BeneпђБts from Adaptive Wasserstein Radius
In the previous parts, I investigated the impacts from choosing a пђБxed Wasserstein
radius. It was seen that it led to increased out-of-sample beneпђБts for DRC-W RAERM
with sample sizes lower than or equal to N = 252. In this section, a dynamic approach
is adopted. The models are evaluated with an approach that combines the ones used
in the experimental section 7 of Esfahani and Kuhn (2015) and experimental design of
Strub and Baumann (2017). In Esfahani and Kuhn (2015), they use a hold-out method
to choose the optimal value for the Wasserstein radius вАШ, and then they test the out-of-
sample performance on new independent samples constructed by simulation. In Strub and
Baumann (2017), they use a rolling-window evaluation where they set aside part of the
historical scenarios as the testing data. The size of the validation sets are chosen to be
approximately 15% of the training size N. Hence, when N = 252, then the size of the
validation set is TV = 37. Figure 5.11 illustrates the combined procedure.

3

Figure 5.11: A total of E historical periods are considered. Each window has a size
of TTR + TV + TTE, where the training data constitute TTR samples, the validation set
constitute TV samples, and the testing data constitute TTE samples. The displacement ¬Ј is
incremented from 0 to E вЙ†TTR + TV + TTE. Due to computational complexity, the пђБxed
increments of ¬Ј are chosen such that 200 rolling windows are evaluated. Together these
form performance samples that can provide statistics about the model performance.
==PAGE:81==
5.3. BENEFITS FROM ADAPTIVE WASSERSTEIN RADIUS
67
5.3.1
Impact on Portfolio Performance for Adaptive Wasserstein
Radius
For the пђБxed Wasserstein radii, it was seen that speciпђБc choices lead to improved
performance, while others led to inferior performance. For W-DRC RAERM with N =
126, Figure 5.9 indicates that вАШ = 10вЙ†1.5 will yield to the best possible average performance.
However, it is not given that the best Wasserstein radius вАШ remains constant in time. In
some periods, high market volatility might increase the uncertainty about the underlying
distribution. This could potentially lead to a situation where the portfolio manager is
better off by hedging amongst a larger set of candidate distributions by increasing the
Wasserstein radius вАШ. Therefore, a hold-out method is adopted.

Figure 5.12: This пђБgure shows the mean value of the objective in out-of-sample in 200

SpeciпђБcally, by optimizing the portfolios over a range of Wasserstein radii вАШ, and then
evaluate them on the validation set, the one that is expected to yield the best possible
performance on the testing set can be selected. After selecting a speciпђБc вАШ, the portfolio is
re-optimized on the entire training set of size N with this speciпђБc choice. Increasing the
validation set might lead to better estimates for the optimal value of вАШ, but it simultaneously
decreases the amount of available training samples. This represents a clear trade-off
==PAGE:82==
68
CHAPTER 5. EVALUATION OF PORTFOLIO MODELS
dilemma. The impact of this speciпђБc trade-off has not been investigated, but it cannot be
ruled out that there is additional performance to be gained by analyzing this.
Figure 5.12 shows the the out-of-sample performance measured in the terms of the
objective value on the testing set under 200 evaluations across the entire dataset. It is
seen that the out-of-sample performance for W-DRC is better than or equal to the SAA
approach under the constructed hold-out scheme. In fact, it suggests that marginal beneпђБts
can be obtain for N = 315 and N = 378, which was previously ruled out. In addition, it
is seen that the average objective value is lower than the minima indicated by Figure 5.9.
This suggests that an adaptive approach by the hold-out method is valuable in the portfolio
optimization setup.

good performance.

5.4
Backtesting of Models
In this section, I conduct a backtest of both models with quarterly rebalancing. The
performance is visualized and compared on relevant metrics. I start by outlining the
proposed metrics, and then the backtests of the two models are analyzed separately.
==PAGE:86==
72
CHAPTER 5. EVALUATION OF PORTFOLIO MODELS
The portfolio models are backtested with quarterly rebalancing starting from 02/01-
2002 with the пђБnal rebalancing being on 03/07-2023. The rebalancing occurs on the пђБrst
trading day in each quarter, which is shortly after the quarterly announced adjustments to
S&P 500. In total, this leads to a total of 87 adjustments to the portfolio. The dataset used
is SP500RawFull. This mean that the considered stocks change from period to period,
which reпђВects to the best possible extend, given the quality of the data, an actual setting
where adjustments are made to the index constituents. Despite selecting the constituents
dynamically, the intersection is still found for each training and testing period. The reason
for this is that prices are often not available upon exit due to bankruptcy, acquisition, or
general delisting. The number of stocks available at each rebalancing for N = 252 can be
seen in Figure A.1 in the appendix.
5.4.1

In order to provide general insight into the advantages and disadvantages of using
the proposed models, meaningful metrics for comparison are needed. Let ri(w) = ЋЖвАЇT

w0 denote the weights at the start of a given period, and let ЋЖr1 denote the returns on all the
assets from market close on day 0 to market close on day 1. Then the weights are updated

Although varying the excess return requirement вАУ, the risk-aversion parameter пђВ, and

of SAA and W-DRC, the backtest is only carried out for вАУ = 5% annually, пђВ= 0.2, and
вАФ = 0.80. The Wasserstein radii вАШ, considered on 50 equally spaced points in interval 10вЙ†9
to 101, are chosen with a hold-out procedure using 15% of the training size. Limiting the
==PAGE:89==

Figure 5.17 visualizes the tracking ability of the ITM model for respectively the SAA
and W-DRC variants. The results for N = 63 and N = 504 corresponding to respectively
a quarter of a year and two years are emphasised as they represent respectively small N
and large N. For N = 504, the trajectories for SAA and W-DRC coincide. It is seen that
the performance is very sensitive to the choice of training size N. The tendency is that
increasing N leads to a pessimistic portfolio construction yielding results that are below
the enhanced index on average, which is further supported when the entire spectrum of
training sizes N are considered. This tendency is depicted in Figure A.8 in the appendix.
On the other hand, the non-stationary nature of пђБnancial markets speaks in favor of limiting
the selected historical data considered, because recent market movements better reпђВect the
current state of the market. Therefore, this пђБnding is not particularly surprising. To gain
a more clear view of the drift away from the enhanced index, the excess returns over the
benchmark have been included in Figure 5.18. The remaining sample sizes are shown in
Figure A.9 in the appendix.

5.4.3
Backtest of W-DRC RAERM
As the goal is to пђБnd the portfolio yielding the best possible risk-adjusted excess
return above S&P 500, selecting вАУ = 0% annually is appropriate. This means the regular
index is considered. To narrow the investigation, the backtest is initially carried out for
пђВ= 2 and вАФ = 0.80, which aligns with the selected parameters in Section 7.1 of Esfahani
and Kuhn (2015). The Wasserstein radii вАШ are chosen from 50 equidistant points in the
log-domain of the interval 10вЙ†7 to 101 using 15% of the training size N in the hold-out
method. Results are visualized for N = 63 and N = 504 such that small and large training
sizes are represented. As mentioned in the backtest section of ITM, different choices for
risk-aversion parameter пђВand the percentile вАФ could lead to different results. Therefore, in
the пђБnally part, the performance for different choices of risk aversion пђВis considered to
limit the risk of drawing wrong conclusions.
Figure 5.19 shows the performance in terms of cumulative returns over the entire period.

Figure 5.19: This пђБgure shows the performance of SAA RAERM and W-DRC RAERM
against S&P 500 for sample sizes N = 63 and N = 504.
Both models deliver impressive excess returns, but as transaction costs, although they are
limited in the S&P 500 market, are omitted, their performances on an absolute scale are
exaggerated. The most signiпђБcant driver for these exaggerated returns are probably the
inherited survival bias from the SP500RawFull, although the dynamic selection limits this
effect compared to SP500RawIntersect. Figure A.1 shows that the number of constituents
range from 340 in the beginning of the period to 465, which indicates that the presence
of the survival bias effect is probably strongest in the beginning of the period and then
gradually weakens.
The overall impression is that both SAA and W-DRC perform similar in terms of
excess returns. In addition, it appears that increasing N leads to a lower excess return.
This is further supported by considering the remaining training sizes N, which are shown
in Figure A.17. A possible explanation for this phenomenon is the momentum anomaly,
which is a market anomaly пђБrst treated in Jegadeesh and Titman (1993). It states that
near-past winners tend to generate abnormal average returns in the near-future. Increasing
N would therefore lead to an investment strategy less biased towards momentum, which
explains why the effect weakens. The effect also motivated the extension of the Fama-
French three factor model to a пђБve factor model, where Fama and French (2014) show that
the effect is signiпђБcant with regards to explaining average excess returns.
To better assess the differences between respectively the SAA and the W-DRC variant
of RAERM, both the cumulative returns and compounded excess returns above S&P 500
are shown in Figure 5.20. It appears that W-DRC stabilizes the excess returns. The effect
is mostly visible for N = 63 and appear to be diminishing for larger choices of N. Similar
пђБgures for the other choices of N can be seen in Figures A.18 and A.19.

inversely related to the ability to capture excess returns, which has been attributed to the
momentum effect. Hence, if the investor seek to capture this momentum effect to a large

be considered relative to other sample sizes N and not as stand-alone.
Distribution of Performance Metrics
The performance metrics have until now been considered on an average basis. Although
it was hypothesized that the advantage of using W-DRC is driven by a better ability to
reduce the excess risk, a more detailed view can be obtain by considering the violin plot
of the out-of-sample statistics. Figure 5.21 shows the distribution of AER, CVaRвАФ, and
Sortino ratios for respectively SAA/W-DRC RAERM. The plot indicates a signiпђБcant
advantage in terms of reduction of excess risk by using W-DRC for N = 63, while
SAA should be the preferred choice for N = 504. It can also be concluded from the
plot that W-DRC does not lead to superior returns relative to SAA. This implies that
the near-future distributions can only be hedged against downside excess risk with the
adopted methodology. The excess returns, as measured by both TER and AER, are largely

Sensitivity to Risk-Aversion
As mentioned in the above, varying the risk-aversion parameter пђВmight affect the
degree to which W-DRC improve the performance of the portfolios. The excess return
requirement and considered percentile are kept at respectively вАУ = 0% annually and
вАФ = 0.8. The Wasserstein radii вАШ are chosen from 50 equidistant points in the log-domain
of the interval 10вЙ†6 to 101 using 15% of the training size N in the hold-out method.
Results are visualized for N = 63 and N = 504 such that small and large training sizes
are represented.
Figure 5.22 consider the out-of-sample AER and CVaRвАФ for 30 risk aversion parameters
пђВin the range 10вЙ†2 to 100.7. The values N = 63 and N = 252 have been chosen because
previous results indicate that there is a substantial difference for these training sizes
between the results produced for respectively W-DRC and SAA. The setup resembles
that of the efпђБcient frontier considered in Figure 2.2 in the section on background theory.
Although the results do not yield a smooth curve, the typical trade-off between risk and

CHAPTER 6
Conclusion
In this thesis, I have investigated how a portfolio constructed with Wasserstein-based
distributional robustness (W-DRC) can be used to respectively outperform S&P 500 and
track enhanced versions of it. The models were tested on a dataset comprising 23 years of
prices collected from the constituents of S&P 500, and the results were compared to the
sample average approximation (SAA) approach. More speciпђБcally, I set out to answer the
following research questions:
(i) In a single-period setting with large-scale real data, how does reformulating portfolio
optimization tasks with distributional robustness impact the portfolio composition
and its out-of-sample performance? Is there an optimal amount of historical data to
consider for maximized effectiveness?
(ii) Does backtesting, both when the target is to track an enhanced version of an index
and beat it in a risk-adjusted sense, provide supportive evidence for the effectiveness
of distributionally robust portfolio strategies?
The empirical results with пђБxed Wasserstein radii вАШ provide evidence of the ability to
increase out-of-sample performance with W-DRC relative to SAA on the speciпђБc dataset.
For the return-based index tracking model (ITM) with risk aversion пђВ= 0.2 and вАФ = 0.8,
the increased performance is obtained whenever a quarter of past data is used in the
construction. For the risk-adjusted excess return model (RAERM) with risk aversion пђВ= 2
and вАФ = 0.8, the advantage is obtained whenever the portfolios are constructed using
1 year or less of historical data. The advantage of using W-DRC over SAA is found to
be diminishing for increasing training sizes N eventually leading to similar performance
between SAA and W-DRC. Increasing the number of historical samples N considered in
the portfolio constructions improve the objective functions in general. This lead me to
conclude that an optimal amount of past data does not exist, and therefore as much data
as possible should be considered in the portfolio construction. These пђБndings are in line
85
==PAGE:100==
86
CHAPTER 6. CONCLUSION
with the conclusions in Esfahani and Kuhn (2015), where the authors consider portfolios
constructed on assets, whose returns were drawn from normal distributions.
Departing from a пђБxed choice of the Wasserstein radius вАШ, a hold-out method is used
to adaptively пђБnd the optimal choice of radius. Separate backtests are carried out for
respectively ITM and RAERM using quarterly rebalancing. The conducted experiments
do not indicate that a W-DRC ITM generally improve the tracking capability over SAA
ITM. Marginal improvements are obtained when a quarter of past data is used in the
construction, but the effect is absent when considering longer periods. Similar to the
criticism put forward in Strub and Baumann (2017), the return-based formulation did not
seem to lead to low tracking errors as measured by total excess returns (TER). The most
convincing reason is that it does not take dynamic portfolio weights into account during
the construction phase, and W-DRC does not mitigate this issue.
The results of the backtests provide evidence that using W-DRC RAERM over SAA
RAERM can be useful. Delivering similar excess returns over S&P 500, portfolios
constructed with W-DRC has signiпђБcantly lower excess risk for smaller sample sizes N,
which allows the decision-maker to keep the momentum-effect, while simultaneously
decreasing the associated risk relative to the benchmark. As for ITM, the effect of using
W-DRC over SAA dissipates for larger choices of past samples N. A sensitivity analysis to
the risk aversion parameter пђВshow that the advantage of using W-DRC and SAA depends
on the speciпђБc investor proпђБle. In several cases it can be a disadvantage to employ W-DRC
over SAA, which means that the choice must be made from case to case.
Throughout the empirical section it is argued that the intrinsic пђВaws of the used dataset
affects the results. For the relative comparison between SAA and W-DRC formulations it
is deemed unproblematic, but it makes it impossible to translate the results into expected
absolute performance in a setting where the decision-maker has access to a пђВawless dataset.
6.1
Further Research
The conducted analyses use historical prices in the construction of portfolios. This is
valid point of criticism, because the efпђБcient market hypothesis as introduced in Fama and
Malkiel (1970) states that prices fully reпђВect all available information on the companies.
Therefore, the quality of the analysis could be improved by considering the introduced
scheme on forecasts produced by various statistical factor models or even deep learning
architectures such as transformers.
The conducted analyses are kept simple in the thesis, and therefore portfolio constraints
are ignored. This is mainly for the sake of keeping the computational complexity as low as
possible. Suppose the decision-maker is a minor investor, which faces signiпђБcant пђБxed
==PAGE:101==
6.1. FURTHER RESEARCH
87
transaction costs and has no possibility of managing the rebalancing of a portfolio of more
than K assets. If this cardinality constraint is imposed on the optimization task, optimizerвАЩs
course is expected to be more pervasive, because the tendency to select overestimated
assets is ampliпђБed. In such a case, it would be reasonable to expect that the W-DRC
formulation could have increased relative beneпђБts. As the problem then needs to be
modelled by a large-scale mixed integer program, it begs for the usage of heuristics to
reduce the amount of candidate assets to a manageable subset. The primary challenges lies
in making sure that assets that are part of the optimal cardinality constrained portfolio are
not excluded during this phase. Existing literature addresses these issues, and a potential
starting point could be the incorporation of some of the methods outlined in Gilli and
Schumann (2012).

B.3
Par Yields
In par yields, we take a different approach. Instead of specifying the rate that makes
the present value of the cash пђВow equal to the market value, we determine the coupon c
that makes the present value of the cash пђВows equal to par (in our example par is $100).
To carry out this procedure, we need to know the zero-coupon rates for all maturities for
which cash пђВows are exchanged. Suppose we have zero-coupon rates as speciпђБed in B.2.

bond yield, but for short maturities they are almost equivalent. This is why the 3 month
par yield rate is considered as the investment possibility for the bank account.
