APPENDIX C
Duality Theory
Duality has several meanings in literature. It might refer to Lagrangian duality, Wolfe
duality, or Fenchel duality among others. In this thesis, the term exclusively refers to
Lagrangian duality. It is a corner stone in optimization as it occasionally leads to short cuts
in the search for tractable solutions to complicated problems. In the context of distribu-
tionally robust optimization (DRO), it is used to reduce an inпђБnitely constrained program
into a tractable пђБnite program.
C.1
DeпђБning the Dual Problem (DP)
Consider a general optimization problem of the form
min
x≈УRn f(x)
(C.1a)
s.t. ci(x) √Ш 0 for i ≈У I
(C.1b)
ci(x) = 0 for i ≈У E
(C.1c)
where x ≈У Rn are called the decision variables. As stated in Boyd (2009), the idea
of duality theory is to пђБnd and analyse a problem related to the primal problem (original
problem) that can give us insight on the lower bound (or upper bounds) of the problem of
interest. Under certain conditions, the solution to the dual problem reveals the solution to
the primal problem. This key feature is known as strong duality.
In order to deпђБne the dual program, we need to deпђБne the Lagrangian L. It consists of
the objective and a weighted sum of the equality and inequality constraints.

C.3
Lower Bound with Dual Program
The dual objective at optimality gives a lower bound on the minimization problem (or
upper bound on the maximization problem). To provide a formal argument, suppose that
x is a feasible solution to the primal problem given in Program C.7. Then the inequality
constraint is satisпђБed.
Ax √Ж b ¬° (Ax вЙ†b) √Ж 0

respectively the primal and dual problem. This proves the claim. In fact, the result also
holds when the original problem is non-convex (Boyd (2009)).
C.4
Strong Duality
In Equation C.13, we established the relative order of the objective functions of the
primal and the dual problem. Suppose that вБД√Ї is optimal with respect to the dual problem,
and x√Ї is optimal with respect to the primal problem. The difference вЙ†cTx√Ї вЙ†(вЙ†bTвБД√Ї)
is referred to as the duality gap. If SlaterвАЩs constraint qualiпђБcations hold, and the primal
problem is convex (i.e., it has convex objective and convex constraints), it can be shown
that the duality gap is 0. This implies that the two problems are equivalent, which is what
we exploit in the derivation of the Wasserstein-based DRO counterpart. A formal proof of
strong duality can be found in Boyd (2009) on page 234.

APPENDIX E
Mixture-Based Distributionally
Robust Counterpart
The ambiguity set speciпђБed by the Wasserstein ball in 2.45 leaves room for all sorts of
valid probability distributions. One way to impose a certain structure was to conпђБne the
support set ќЮ to a polyhedral, which could e.g. be the convex hull of the observations. It is
needless to say that specifying the structure of the support set in the context of portfolio
optimization requires bold assumptions, and even if these can be justiпђБed, it increases the
computational complexity multiple folds. It turns out that there is another way to restrict
the family of candidate distributions even further.
E.1
Statement of Corollary 1 in Gao and Kleywegt (2017)
Consider the mixture-based ambiguity set as deпђБned in 2.46. We now impose that
the metric d is a Wasserstein metric with the norm p, where the reference distribution P
is chosen to be the empirical probability distribution ЋЖPN. As previously, q refers to the
dual norm of p such that for p = 1, we get q = ≈Т. We therefore get the more speciпђБc
mixture-based ambiguity set as follows:
PMIX = P = {Q ≈У M(ќЮ) | dq
W(Q, ЋЖPN) < вАШ, EQ[(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)(вАЇ вЙ†ЋЖ¬µ)T 4 вАЬ ЋЖќ£}
(E.1)
To the best of their knowledge, the authors of Gao and Kleywegt (2017) are the пђБrst
ones to incorporate an ambiguity set of the form presented in E.1. The general DRO
problem now takes the following form:

Let вАШ be the predetermined Wasserstein radius, let ЋЖќ£ be an estimate of the covariance

APPENDIX F
Wasserstein-Based
Distributionally Robust
Counterpart
As has been discussed in the main content of the thesis, we need a way to enforce
the behavior of a natures role in trying to oppose our task of achieving a good outcome
of our optimization problem under uncertainty. In distributionally robust optimization
(DRO), this amounts to solving an inner maximization program, which seeks to choose a
probability distribution from an ambiguity set P making the task as difпђБcult as possible.
The following section provides the proof of Corollary 5.1 on page 139 in Esfahani and
Kuhn (2015). The derivations require a few assumptions, which are stated below. The
пђБrst is about convexity of the uncertainty set and the structure of the loss functions, which
corresponds to Assumption 4.1 on page 128 of Esfahani and Kuhn (2015).
Assumption 3 (Convexity). Let K = {1, . . . , K}. The uncertainty set ќЮ вДҐRM, where
the uncertainties вАЇ reside, is convex and closed, and the negative constituent functions
вЙ†ak are proper, convex, and lower semicontinuous for all k ≈У K. Moreover, assume
that ak is not identically вЙ†≈Т on ќЮ for all k ≈У K.
In addition, it should be mentioned that although utilizing the maximum operator in
place of the supremum (and the same for minimum and inпђБmum) is the correct thing to do,
it is assumed that they can be used interchangeably.
133

F.1. PROOF OF COROLLARY 5.1 IN Esfahani and Kuhn (2015)
135
Having settled on the reference distribution ЋЖPN, we need to specify the concept of
closeness. In literature, the Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence as a distance measure
(not a metric) has received a lot of attention. There are however certain properties of
the divergence measure making it unsuitable as a choice in deпђБning the ambiguity set.
As discussed by Esfahani and Kuhn (2015) on page 119, one of the major drawbacks
is that the true probability distribution P, assuming it is absolutely continuous, resides
inside the ball with probability 0, whenever the reference distribution is deпђБned as the
empirical distribution on a пђБnite amount of samples. Naturally, this render the use of KL
divergence meaningless in this context. An alternative, which guarantees that the empirical
distribution ЋЖPN resides within the ambiguity set constructed with a reasonable choice of
radius вАШ, is the Wasserstein distance metric. Using DeпђБnition 3.1 in Esfahani and Kuhn
(2015) on page 122, the Wasserstein distance is deпђБned as
dW(Q1, Q2) = inf
Q {EQ[√ОвАЇ1 вЙ†вАЇ2√О] | Q1(вАЇ1) =
вБД
вАЇ2≈УќЮ dQ(вАЇ1, вАЇ2),

вБД
вАЇ1≈УќЮ dQ(вАЇ1, вАЇ2)}

as an instance of an optimal transportation problem, where the decision variables are Q. In
this context, Q holds the information of how to move the probability mass from Q1 to Q2
(or vice versa). Let ќЮV denote the пђБnite uncertainty set which deпђБne the sample space of
our random vectors вАЇ.
ќЮV = {вАЇ1, . . . , вАЇV }
(F.8)

BвАШ(ЋЖPN) = P = P(вАШ) := {Q ≈У M(ќЮV ) : dW(ЋЖPN, Q) √Ж вАШ}

common to approximate those with piecewise afпђБne functions. This will in many cases
even lead to more efпђБcient programs.

polytope. This means it has the structure ќЮ = {вАЇ ≈У RM : CвАЇ √Ж d}, where C is a

G.3
Portfolio Constraints
As was mentioned in the background theory section, portfolio managers face several
portfolio constraints. Assume a feasible portfolio w0 is available. The focus is then on
modelling the changes to the optimal portfolio w. Let ЋЬw denote the amount of stocks
purchased or sold during the initial or subsequent rebalances.
ЋЬw + w0 = w
(G.39)
In order work with nonnegative variables, the variables w+, wвЙ†√Ш 0 are introduced.
w+ вЙ†wвЙ†= ЋЬw
(G.40)
To deal with transaction costs, which will be introduced next, binary vectors y+ and
yвЙ†are introduced to indicate whether an asset has been purchased or sold. Naturally, not
both can be 1 at the same time. This consideration gives rise to the following constraint.
y+ + yвЙ†√Ж 1
(G.41)
For the sake of clarity, y+, yвЙ†≈У {0, 1}n and w+, wвЙ†, ЋЬw, w ≈У Rn.
G.3.1
Transaction Costs
In order to introduce transaction costs, it is assumed that trades can be carried out at
the adjusted closing price supplied to the model. Let f +, f вЙ†≈У Rn denote the пђБxed prices
associated with respectively purchasing and selling assets. Similarly, let v+, vвЙ†≈У Rn
denote the variable costs associated with the same transactions. In addition, assume that
the rebalancing is self-пђБnancing meaning all purchases or sales are fully funded by the
account balance. When dealing with fractions, this means that it has to add up to 1.
==PAGE:166==
152
APPENDIX G. MODERN PORTFOLIO THEORY
1Tw вЙ†(√Иf +, y+√Н + √Иf вЙ†, yвЙ†√Н + √Иv+, w+√Н + √ИvвЙ†, wвЙ†√Н)
¬Є
ЋЪЋЩ
ЋЭ

= 1
(G.42a)
w+ √Ж My+
(G.42b)

(G.42d)
y+, yвЙ†≈У {0, 1}n
(G.42e)
If the amount of turnover increases, then w is forced to be smaller yielding less
attractive returns. The constant M is used to make sure that the decision variables are
unconstrained whenever penalized with an activation of entries in respectively y+ and yвЙ†.
Depending on the application, M should not need to be larger than 1, which corresponds
to buying or selling 100% of a speciпђБc asset. Equation G.42 is often referred to as the
self-пђБnancing constraint.
G.3.2
Minimum Transaction Amounts
Although the model decides that the optimal rebalancing occurs with ЋЬw, investors
might be interested in capping purchases and sales from below to avoid arbitrary small
trades.
w+ √Ш K1 ¬І y+
(G.43a)

(G.43b)

(G.43d)
In the above, ¬І denote the element-wise product between the minimum transaction
amount vectors K1, K2 ≈У (0, 1)n.
G.3.3
Undertakings Collective Investment in Transferable Securities
(UCITS)
The UCITS directive is a regulatory framework allowing funds, which comply with
the requirements and receive authorisation from a single member state of EU to operate
freely within the market. The purpose is to introduce transparency in order to obtain high
consumer protection. A central requirement in the regulatory framework is the 5/10/40
==PAGE:167==
G.3. PORTFOLIO CONSTRAINTS
153
rule. It states that no single position can constitute more than 10% of the total assets.
Amongst those positions that exceed 5% of the holdings of the fund, the aggregate share
must not exceed 40%. As previously, let w denote the percentage of holdings in each
security wi for i ≈У {1, . . . , n}. Let u be a UCITS selector such that entry ui is 1 if an only
if wi √Ш 0.05.
w √Ж 0.10
(G.44a)
вЙ†w √Ж 0
(G.44b)
w √Ж 0.05 + y ¬Ј 0.05
(G.44c)
wTy √Ж 0.40
(G.44d)
w ≈У Rn
(G.44e)
y ≈У {0, 1}n
(G.44f)
The problem with the formulation in Equation G.44 is that it contains a bilinear
constraint. We can linearize the bilinear constraint as follows. Let zi replace wiyi. Then z
is a vector of the same dimensions as w and y.
вЙ†z √Ж 0
(G.45a)
z √Ж y ¬Ј 0.10
(G.45b)
z √Ж w
(G.45c)
вЙ†z √Ж вЙ†w вЙ†(0 вЙ†0.10)(1 вЙ†y)
(G.45d)
1Tz √Ж 0.40
(G.45e)
w ≈У Rn
(G.45f)
y ≈У {0, 1}n
(G.45g)
z ≈У Rn
(G.45h)
The пђБrst two equations of Equation G.45 ensures that if yi is 0, then zi is equal to 0.
Then last two equations of G.45 makes sure that yi = 1 implies that zi = wi. Combining
the пђБndings gives us the following constraints to enforce the 5/10/40 rule on the portfolio.
==PAGE:168==
154
APPENDIX G. MODERN PORTFOLIO THEORY
w √Ж 0.10
(G.46a)
вЙ†w √Ж 0
(G.46b)
w √Ж 0.05 + y ¬Ј 0.05
(G.46c)
вЙ†z √Ж 0
(G.46d)
z √Ж y ¬Ј 0.10
(G.46e)
z √Ж w
(G.46f)
вЙ†z √Ж вЙ†w вЙ†(0 вЙ†0.10)(1 вЙ†y)
(G.46g)
1Tz √Ж 0.40
(G.46h)
w ≈У Rn
(G.46i)
y ≈У {0, 1}n
(G.46j)
z ≈У Rn
(G.46k)
Hence, at the expense of introducing n new continuous decision variables, we were
able to reduce the computational complexity of the program signiпђБcantly.
